[1mูุฑุงุกุงุช ุฅุถุงููุฉ [0m

There are many books on the subjects of nations and nationalism; and, indeed, new ones appear almost daily. As a consequence, it 
is difficult to draw attention to even the more important ones without overlooking a number of others worthy of careful 
consideration, especially covering subjects that are so controversial. Thus, in addition to the works listed in the references to 
each chapter, I have, in what follows, provided a brief overview of a few of the more important books on nations and nationalism. 

Historical works on a particular nation are almost as old as our first written records; certainly, there are examples from 
antiquity such as Josephusโs [3mThe Antiquities of the Jews [0m. Nevertheless, one can say that the scholarly study of the nation, as a 
problem to be explained, began in the latter half of the 18th century with two works by Johann Gottfried von Herder, [3mYet Another 
Philosophy of History for the Education of Mankind [0m(New York, 1968) and [3mReflections on the Philosophy of the History of Mankind [0m
(Chicago, 1968). For a critical evaluation of these works, see Freidrich Meinecke, [3mHistorism [0m(London, 1972); and Steven Grosby, 
โHerderโs Theory of the Nationโ in [3mEncyclopaedia of Nationalism [0m, ed. Athena S. Leoussi (New Brunswick, 2001). 

Discussions of the nation appeared with greater frequency in the 19th century. Some of the more noteworthy were Johann Gottlieb 
Fichte, [3mAddresses to the German Nation [0m(New York, 1968); Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel, [3mThe Philosophy of History [0m(New York, 
1956); John Emerich Edward Dalberg-Acton, โNationalityโ in [3mEssays in the History of Liberty [0m(Indianapolis, 1986); and especially 
Ernest Renan, โWhat is a Nation?โ in [3mThe Poetry of the Celtic Races and Other Studies [0m(London, 1896). 

Among the many works on the nation written in the aftermath of World War I, important were Freidrich Meinecke, [3mCosmopolitanism and 
the National State [0m(Princeton, 1970); Johan Huizinga, โPatriotism and Nationalism in European Historyโ in [3mMen and Ideas [0m
(Princeton, 1984); Carlton Hayes, [3mEssays on Nationalism [0m(New York, 1926); and [3mThe Historical Evolution of Modern Nationalism [0m(New 
York, 1931). It was during this period that works by the most prolific, until recently, writer on nationalism, Hans Kohn, began to 
appear. 

During and immediately following World War II, attempts to understand the nation, nationalism, fascism, and the political 
movements for national independence resulted in numerous works. Significant contributions during this period would include Hans 
Kohn, [3mThe Idea of Nationalism [0m(New York, 1943); [3mThe Age of Nationalism [0m(New York, 1962); Frederick Hertz, [3mNationality in History 
and Politics [0m(London, 1944); Louis Snyder, [3mThe Meaning of Nationalism [0m(New Brunswick, 1954); Karl Deutsch, [3mNationalism and Social 
Communication [0m(Cambridge, 1953); Elie Kedourie, [3mNationalism [0m(London, 1960); [3mNationalism in Asia and Africa [0m(New York, 1970); and 
Hugh Seton-Watson, [3mNations and States [0m(London, 1977). Sophisticated analyses of nations now appeared by ancient and medieval 
historians, for example Frank Walbank, โThe Problem of Greek Nationalityโ and โNationality as a Factor in Roman Historyโ, in [3m
Selected Papers [0m(Cambridge, 1985); Ernest Kantorowicz, โPro Patria Mori in Medieval Political Thoughtโ, [3mThe American Historical 
Review [0mLVI/3 (April 1951): 472โ92; Gaines Post, โTwo Notes on Nationalism in the Middle Agesโ, [3mTraditio [0mIX (1953): 281โ320; and 
Joseph Strayer, โFrance: The Holy Land, the Chosen People, and the Most Christian Kingโ, in [3mMedieval Statecraft and the 
Perspectives of History [0m(Princeton, 1971). 

Furthermore, during this period there appeared a number of important works on the ideology of nationalism by intellectual 
historians such as George Mosse, [3mThe Crisis of German Ideology [0m(New York, 1964). 

In the last 25 years, significant works on the nation have included John Armstrong, [3mNations Before Nationalism [0m(Chapel Hill, 
1982); Dominique Schnapper, [3mCommunity of Citizens [0m(New Brunswick, 1998); Adrian Hastings, [3mThe Constitution of Nationhood [0m
(Cambridge, 1997); Steven Grosby, [3mBiblical Ideas of Nationality: Ancient and Modern [0m(Winona Lake, 2002); John Hutchinson, [3mNations 
as Zones of Conflict [0m(London, 2005). Worthy of careful attention are the books by the most prolific and thoughtful writer on this 
subject during this period, Anthony D. Smith, [3mThe Ethnic Origin of Nations [0m(Oxford, 1986); [3mNationalism and Modernism [0m(London, 
1998); [3mMyths and Memories of the Nation [0m(Oxford, 1999); [3mThe Nation in History [0m(Hanover, 2000); [3mChosen Peoples [0m(Oxford, 2004); and [3m
The Antiquity of Nations [0m(Cambridge, 2004). 

Other influential, recent books would include Benedict Anderson, [3mImagined Communities [0m(London, 1983); Ernest Gellner, [3mNations and 
Nationalism [0m(Ithaca, 1983); John Breuilly, [3mNationalism and the State [0m(Chicago, 1982); and Liah Greenfeld, [3mNationalism: Five Roads 
to Modernity [0m(Cambridge, 1992). There are also numerous case studies of high quality, such as John Hutchinson, [3mThe Dynamics of 
Cultural Nationalism: The Gaelic Revival and the Creation of the Irish National State [0m(London, 1987); and works on the relation of 
nations and nationalism to other human activities, such as George Mosse, [3mNationalism and Sexuality [0m(Madison, 1985); Athena 
Leoussi, [3mNationalism and Classicism [0m(New York, 1998); and to philosophy, David Miller, [3mOn Nationality [0m(Oxford, 1995).
